908 <section id="_1">

909

910 <h2>

911General Information About Adult Central Nervous System (CNS) Tumors</h2>

912

913 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

914<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

915<h6>

916In This Section</h6>

917<ul>

929</li>

930<li>

933</li>

946</a>

954<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

956Go to Patient Version</a>

957</div>

958<section id="_619">

959<h3 id="_619_toc">

960Incidence and Mortality</h3>

961<p id="_933" tabindex="-1">

962Brain tumors account for 85% to 90% of all primary central nervous system (CNS) tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9631</a>

964] Estimated new cases and deaths from brain tumors and other nervous system tumors in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9652</a>

966]</p>

967<div class="pdq-content-list">

968<ul id="_934">

969<li>

970New cases: 24,810.</li>

971<li>

972Deaths: 18,990.</li>

973</ul>

974</div>

975<p id="_935" tabindex="-1">

976 Data from the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program database for 2015 to 2019 indicated that the combined incidence of brain and other CNS tumors in the United States was 6.3 per 100,000 people per year. Data for 2016 to 2020 indicated an estimated mortality rate of 4.4 deaths per 100,000 people per year.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9773</a>

978] Worldwide, approximately 308,102 new cases of brain and other CNS tumors were diagnosed in the year 2020, with an estimated 251,329 deaths.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9794</a>

980]</p>

981<p id="_1036" tabindex="-1">

982In general, the incidence of primary CNS tumors is higher in White individuals than in Black individuals, and mortality is higher in males than in females.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9833</a>

984]</p>

985<p id="_1037" tabindex="-1">

986Primary brain tumors include the following in decreasing order of frequency:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9871</a>

988]</p>

989<div class="pdq-content-list">

990<ul id="_1038">

991<li>

992Anaplastic astrocytomas and glioblastomas (38% of primary brain tumors).</li>

993<li>

994Meningiomas and other mesenchymal tumors (27% of primary brain tumors).</li>

995<li>

996Pituitary tumors.</li>

997<li>

998Schwannomas.</li>

999<li>

1000CNS lymphomas.</li>

1001<li>

1002Oligodendrogliomas.</li>

1003<li>

1004Ependymomas.</li>

1005<li>

1006Low-grade astrocytomas.</li>

1007<li>

1008Medulloblastomas.</li>

1009</ul>

1010</div>

1011<p id="_1039" tabindex="-1">

1012Primary spinal tumors include the following in decreasing order of frequency: </p>

1013<div class="pdq-content-list">

1014<ul id="_1040">

1015<li>

1016Schwannomas, meningiomas, and ependymomas (79% of primary spinal tumors).</li>

1017<li>

1018Sarcomas.</li>

1019<li>

1020Astrocytomas.</li>

1021<li>

1022Vascular tumors.</li>

1023<li>

1024Chordomas.</li>

1025</ul>

1026</div>

1027<p id="_1239" tabindex="-1">

1028Primary brain tumors

1029rarely spread to other areas of the body, but they can spread to other parts of

1030the brain and to the spinal axis.</p>

1031</section>

1032<section id="_969">

1033<h3 id="_969_toc">

1034Anatomy</h3>

1035<p id="_1377" tabindex="-1">

1036<figure id="figure_1238" class="image-center-medium">

1037<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/765241.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1038Enlarge</a>

1039<img id="_1238" alt="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorium (the upper part of the brain) and the infratentorium (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorium includes the cerebrum, ventricles (fluid-filled spaces), choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The infratentorium includes the cerebellum and brain stem (pons and medulla). The spinal cord is also shown." title="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorium (the upper part of the brain) and the infratentorium (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorium includes the cerebrum, ventricles (fluid-filled spaces), choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The infratentorium includes the cerebellum and brain stem (pons and medulla). The spinal cord is also shown." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/765241-750.jpg">

1040<figcaption class="caption-container">

1041Anatomy of the inside of the brain. The supratentorium contains the cerebrum, ventricles (with cerebrospinal fluid shown in blue), choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The infratentorium contains the cerebellum and brain stem.</figcaption>

1042</figure>

1043</p>

1044</section>

1045<section id="_895">

1046<h3 id="_895_toc">

1047Risk Factors</h3>

1048<p id="_936" tabindex="-1">

1049Few definitive observations have been made about environmental or occupational causes of primary CNS tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10501</a>

1051] </p>

1052<p id="_1373" tabindex="-1">

1053The following potential risk factors have been considered:</p>

1054<div class="pdq-content-list">

1055<ul id="_1363">

1056<li>

1057Exposure to vinyl chloride may be a risk factor for glioma.</li>

1058<li>

1059Epstein-Barr virus infection has been implicated in the etiology of primary CNS lymphoma.</li>

1060<li>

1061Transplant recipients and patients with AIDS have a substantially increased risk of primary CNS lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10621</a>

1063,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10645</a>

1065] For more information, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/primary-cns-lymphoma-treatment-pdq">

1066Primary CNS Lymphoma Treatment</a>

1067.</li>

1068</ul>

1069</div>

1070<p id="_1041" tabindex="-1">

1071The familial tumor syndromes and related chromosomal abnormalities that are associated with CNS neoplasms include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10726</a>

1073,<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10747</a>

1075]</p>

1076<div class="pdq-content-list">

1077<ul id="_1042">

1078<li>

1079 Neurofibromatosis type 1 (17q11).</li>

1080<li>

1081Neurofibromatosis type 2 (22q12).</li>

1082<li>

1083von Hippel-Lindau disease (3p25-26).</li>

1084<li>

1085Tuberous sclerosis (9q34, 16p13). </li>

1086<li>

1087Li-Fraumeni syndrome (17p13). </li>

1088<li>

1089Turcot syndrome type 1 (3p21, 7p22).</li>

1090<li>

1091Turcot syndrome type 2 (5q21).</li>

1092<li>

1093Nevoid basal cell carcinoma syndrome (9q22.3).</li>

1094</ul>

1095</div>

1096</section>

1097<section id="_902">

1098<h3 id="_902_toc">

1099Clinical Features</h3>

1100<p id="_941" tabindex="-1">

1101The clinical presentation of various brain tumors is best appreciated by considering the relationship of signs and symptoms to anatomy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

11021</a>

1103] </p>

1104<p id="_1364" tabindex="-1">

1105General signs and symptoms include the following:</p>

1106<div class="pdq-content-list">

1107<ul id="_942">

1108<li>

1109 Headaches.</li>

1110<li>

1111 Seizures.</li>

1112<li>

1113Visual changes.</li>

1114<li>

1115Gastrointestinal symptoms such as loss of appetite, nausea, and vomiting.</li>

1116<li>

1117Changes in personality, mood, mental capacity, and concentration.</li>

1118</ul>

1119</div>

1120<p id="_986" tabindex="-1">

1121 Seizures are a presenting symptom in approximately 20% of patients with supratentorial brain tumors and may antedate the clinical diagnosis by months to years in patients with slow-growing tumors. Among all patients with brain tumors, 70% with primary parenchymal tumors and 40% with metastatic brain tumors develop seizures at some time during the clinical course.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11228</a>

1123]</p>

1124</section>

1125<section id="_716">

1126<h3 id="_716_toc">

1127Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1128<p id="_1240" tabindex="-1">

1129All brain tumors, whether primary, metastatic, malignant, or benign, must be differentiated from other space-occupying lesions that can have similar clinical presentations, such as abscesses, arteriovenous malformations, and infarctions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11309</a>

1131]</p>

1132<section id="_896">

1133<h4 id="_896_toc">

1134Imaging tests</h4>

1135<p id="_944" tabindex="-1">

1136Contrast-enhanced computed tomography (CT) and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) have complementary roles in the diagnosis of CNS neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

11371</a>

1138,<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11399</a>

1140,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

114110</a>

1142]</p>

1143<div class="pdq-content-list">

1144<ul id="_1044">

1145<li>

1146 The speed of CT is desirable for evaluating clinically unstable patients. CT is superior for detecting calcifications, skull lesions, and hyperacute hemorrhages (bleeding less than 24 hours old) and helps direct differential diagnosis and immediate management.</li>

1147<li>

1148MRI has superior soft-tissue resolution. MRI can better detect isodense lesions, tumor enhancements, and associated findings such as edema, all phases of hemorrhagic states (except hyperacute), and infarctions. High-quality MRI is the diagnostic study of choice in the evaluation of intramedullary and extramedullary spinal cord lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

11491</a>

1150]</li>

1151</ul>

1152</div>

1153<p id="_1045" tabindex="-1">

1154In posttherapy imaging, single-photon emission computed tomography (SPECT) and positron emission tomography (PET) may be useful in differentiating tumor recurrence from radiation necrosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11559</a>

1156]</p>

1157</section>

1158<section id="_970">

1159<h4 id="_970_toc">

1160Biopsy</h4>

1161<p id="_945" tabindex="-1">

1162Biopsy confirmation to corroborate the suspected diagnosis of a primary brain tumor is critical, whether before surgery by needle biopsy or at the time of surgical resection. The exception is cases in which the clinical and radiological evidence clearly points to a benign tumor, which could potentially be managed with active surveillance without biopsy or treatment. For other cases, radiological patterns may be misleading, and a definitive biopsy is needed to rule out other causes of space-occupying lesions, such as metastatic cancer or infection.</p>

1163<p id="_1046" tabindex="-1">

1164CT- or MRI-guided stereotactic techniques can be used to place a needle safely and accurately into almost all locations in the brain.</p>

1165</section>

1166</section>

1167<section id="_972">

1168<h3 id="_972_toc">

1169Prognostic Factors</h3>

1170<p id="_1341" tabindex="-1">

1171Several genetic alterations have emerged as powerful prognostic factors in diffuse glioma (astrocytoma, oligodendroglioma, mixed glioma, and glioblastoma), and these alterations may guide patient management. Specific alterations include the following:</p>

1172<div class="pdq-content-list">

1173<ul id="_1365">

1174<li>

1175DNA methylation of the <em class="gene-name">

1176MGMT</em>

1177 gene promoter.</li>

1178<li>

1179Mutation of the <em class="gene-name">

1180IDH1</em>

1181 or <em class="gene-name">

1182IDH2</em>

1183 gene.</li>

1184<li>

1185Codeletion of chromosomes 1p and 19q. </li>

1186</ul>

1187</div>

1188<p id="_1384" tabindex="-1">

1189 Other prognostic factors that confer poor prognosis include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

119011</a>

1191,<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

119212</a>

1193]</p>

1194<div class="pdq-content-list">

1195<ul id="_1385">

1196<li>

1197 Age older than 40 years.</li>

1198<li>

1199 Progressive disease. </li>

1200<li>

1201Tumor size larger than 5 cm. </li>

1202<li>

1203Tumor crossing the midline. </li>

1204<li>

1205Contrast enhancement on MRI.</li>

1206<li>

1207World Health Organization performance status (&#8805;1). </li>

1208<li>

1209Neurological symptoms. </li>

1210<li>

1211Less than a gross total resection. </li>

1212</ul>

1213</div>

1214<p id="_1386" tabindex="-1">

1215In an exploratory analysis of 318 patients with low-grade glioma treated with either radiation therapy alone or temozolomide chemotherapy alone, a combination of these prognostic factors demonstrated the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

121611</a>

1217] </p>

1218<div class="pdq-content-list">

1219<ol id="_1387">

1220<li>

1221Longer progression-free survival (PFS) in patients with an <em class="gene-name">

1222IDH</em>

1223 mutation without codeletion of 1p/19q when treated with radiation therapy (hazard ratio, 1.86; 95% confidence interval, 1.21&#8211;2.87; log-rank, <em>

1224P</em>

1225 = .0043). </li>

1226<li>

1227No significant treatment-dependent differences in PFS for patients with an <em class="gene-name">

1228IDH</em>

1229 mutation with codeletion of 1p/19q and <em class="gene-name">

1230IDH</em>

1231 wild-type tumors. </li>

1232<li>

1233Patients with wild-type <em class="gene-name">

1234IDH</em>

1235 tumors had the worst prognosis independent of treatment type. </li>

1236<li>

1237Patients with <em class="gene-name">

1238IDH</em>

1239-mutated tumors with codeletion of 1p/19q had the best prognosis.</li>

1240<li>

1241The O6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase (MGMT) promoter status in low-grade tumors was methylated in:<div class="pdq-content-list">

1242<ul id="_1388">

1243<li>

1244All <em class="gene-name">

1245IDH</em>

1246 mutations with codeletion of 1p/19q (45/45).</li>

1247<li>

1248Most, but not all (86%, 62/72), of the <em class="gene-name">

1249IDH</em>

1250 mutations without codeletion of 1p/19q.</li>

1251<li>

1252Fifty-six percent (5/9) of the <em class="gene-name">

1253IDH</em>

1254 wild-type cases. </li>

1255</ul>

1256</div>

1257</li>

1258</ol>

1259</div>

1260<p id="_1366" tabindex="-1">

1261For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_792">

1262Treatment of Primary Central Nervous System Tumors by Tumor Type</a>

1263 section.</p>

1264</section>

1315

1316

1317 <section id="_5">

1318

1319 <h2>

1320World Health Organization (WHO) Classification of Adult Primary CNS Tumors</h2>

1321

1322 <div id="_section_5" class="pdq-sections">

1323<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1324<h6>

1325In This Section</h6>

1326<ul>

1327<li>

1328<a href="#_1047_toc">

1329Genomic Alterations</a>

1330</li>

1331</ul>

1332</nav>

1333<p id="_257" tabindex="-1">

1334This classification is based on the World Health Organization (WHO) classification of central nervous system (CNS) tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

13351</a>

1336] The WHO approach incorporates and interrelates morphology, cytogenetics, molecular genetics, and immunological markers in an attempt to construct a cellular classification that is universally applicable and prognostically valid. Earlier attempts to develop a TNM (tumor, node, metastasis)-based classification were dropped for the following reasons:[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

13372</a>

1338]</p>

1339<div class="pdq-content-list">

1340<ul id="_1049">

1341<li>

1342Tumor size (T) is less relevant than are tumor histology and location.</li>

1343<li>

1344Nodal status (N) does not apply because the brain and spinal cord have no lymphatics. </li>

1345<li>

1346Metastatic spread (M) rarely applies because most patients with CNS neoplasms do not live long enough to develop metastatic disease.</li>

1347</ul>

1348</div>

1349<p id="_258" tabindex="-1">

1350The WHO grading of CNS tumors establishes a malignancy scale based on histological features of the tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

13513</a>

1352] The histological grades are as follows:</p>

1353<div class="pdq-content-list">

1354<ul id="_980">

1355<li>

1356<strong>

1357WHO grade I</strong>

1358 includes lesions with low proliferative potential, a frequently discrete nature, and the possibility of cure following surgical resection alone. </li>

1359<li>

1360<strong>

1361WHO grade II</strong>

1362 includes lesions that are generally infiltrating and low in mitotic activity but recur more frequently than do grade I malignant tumors after local therapy. Some tumor types tend to progress to higher grades of malignancy.</li>

1363<li>

1364<strong>

1365WHO grade III</strong>

1366 includes lesions with histological evidence of malignancy, including nuclear atypia and increased mitotic activity. These lesions have anaplastic histology and infiltrative capacity. They are usually treated with aggressive adjuvant therapy.</li>

1367<li>

1368<strong>

1369WHO grade IV</strong>

1370 includes lesions that are mitotically active, necrosis prone, and generally associated with a rapid preoperative and postoperative progression and fatal outcomes. The lesions are usually treated with aggressive adjuvant therapy.</li>

1371</ul>

1372</div>

1373<p id="_719" tabindex="-1">

1374Table 1 lists the tumor types and grades.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

13754</a>

1376] Tumors limited to the peripheral nervous system are not included. Histopathology, grading methods, incidence, and what is known about etiology specific to each tumor type have been described in detail elsewhere.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

13774</a>

1378,<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

13795</a>

1380]</p>

2140<section id="_1047">

2141<h3 id="_1047_toc">

2142Genomic Alterations</h3>

2143<p id="_1342" tabindex="-1">

2144Alterations in the <em class="gene-name">

2145BRAF</em>

2146, <em class="gene-name">

2147IDH1</em>

2148, and <em class="gene-name">

2149IDH2</em>

2150 genes, and genomic 1p/19q codeletion, appear to be hallmark aberrations in particular glioma subtypes. Assessment for the presence of these mutations aids diagnosis and prognosis and, with regard to 1p/19q codeletion, predicts for response to chemotherapy. </p>

2151<p id="_1343" tabindex="-1">

2152In pilocytic astrocytomas (WHO grade I), tandem duplication at 7q34 leading to a fusion between <em class="gene-name">

2153KIAA1549</em>

2154 and <em class="gene-name">

2155BRAF</em>

2156 is found in approximately 70% of pilocytic astrocytomas.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

21576</a>

2158-<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

21598</a>

2160] An activating point mutation in <em class="gene-name">

2161BRAF</em>

2162 (V600E) is found in an additional 5% to 9% of these tumors and in general, <em class="gene-name">

2163RAF</em>

2164 alterations occur in approximately 80% of pilocytic astrocytomas.</p>

2165<p id="_1344" tabindex="-1">

2166<em class="gene-name">

2167BRAF </em>

2168 V600E mutations are observed (in about 60%) of other benign glioma variants, including pleomorphic xanthoastrocytoma and ganglioglioma, while <em class="gene-name">

2169BRAF</em>

2170 tandem duplications are not found in these variant glioma tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

21719</a>

2172-<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

217311</a>

2174]</p>

2175<p id="_1345" tabindex="-1">

2176Most WHO grade II and III diffuse gliomas (astrocytomas, oligodendrogliomas, and oligoastrocytomas) and 5% to 10% of glioblastomas (WHO grade IV) harbor point mutations in the R132 position of <em class="gene-name">

2177IDH1</em>

2178 or, rarely, the analogous codon in <em class="gene-name">

2179IDH2 </em>

2180(R172).[<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

218112</a>

2182-<a href="#cit/section_2.16">

218316</a>

2184] The presence of an <em class="gene-name">

2185IDH1</em>

2186 or <em class="gene-name">

2187IDH2</em>

2188 mutation is a strong prognostic factor. Patients with these mutant tumors have significantly longer survival independent of WHO grade or histological subtype.</p>

2189<p id="_1346" tabindex="-1">

2190Deletion of chromosomes 1p and 19q occurs through a translocation event [<a href="#cit/section_2.17">

219117</a>

2192] and is common in oligodendrogliomas. 1p/19q codeletion is a powerful prognostic factor and may predict for response to chemotherapy. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1144">

2193Anaplastic oligodendrogliomas treatment</a>

2194 section.</p>

2195<p id="_1347" tabindex="-1">

2196These genetic alterations have potential diagnostic utility. Presence of the <em class="gene-name">

2197IDH1</em>

2198 and <em class="gene-name">

2199IDH2</em>

2200 mutations may distinguish diffuse gliomas from other glioma variants, which often have <em class="gene-name">

2201BRAF</em>

2202 genetic alterations, and nonneoplastic reactive astrocytosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

220318</a>

2204] Most (90%) <em class="gene-name">

2205IDH</em>

2206 mutations in gliomas result in an R132H substitution, which can be detected with a highly sensitive and specific monoclonal antibody. A rapid immunohistochemical analysis using the mutant-specific <em class="gene-name">

2207IDH1</em>

2208 antibody can aid diagnostic analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

220919</a>

2210]</p>

2211<p id="_1048" tabindex="-1">

2212Other CNS tumors are associated with characteristic patterns of altered oncogenes, altered tumor suppressor genes, and chromosomal abnormalities. Familial tumor syndromes with defined chromosomal abnormalities are associated with gliomas.</p>

2213</section>

Brain Pathol 20 (1): 245-54, 2010.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=19903171&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=19903171&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2285[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2286</li>

2287</ol>

2288</div>

2289

2290 </section>

2291

2297Treatment Option Overview for Adult Primary CNS Tumors</h2>

2332<section id="_720">

2333<h3 id="_720_toc">

2334Primary CNS Tumors</h3>

2335<p id="_1050" tabindex="-1">

2336This section discusses general treatment modalities for primary central nervous system (CNS) tumors. For a description of specific treatment options for each tumor type, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_792">

2337Treatment of Primary Central Nervous System Tumors by Tumor Type</a>

2338 section.</p>

2339<p id="_1051" tabindex="-1">

2340 Radiation therapy and chemotherapy options vary according to histology and anatomic site of the CNS tumor. For glioblastoma, combined modality therapy with resection, radiation, and chemotherapy is standard. Anaplastic astrocytomas, anaplastic oligodendrogliomas, and anaplastic oligoastrocytomas represent only a small proportion of CNS gliomas; therefore, phase III randomized trials restricted to these tumor types are not generally practical. The natural histories of these tumors are variable, depending on histological and molecular factors; therefore, treatment guidelines are evolving. Therapy involving surgically implanted carmustine-impregnated polymer wafers combined with postoperative external-beam radiation therapy (EBRT) may play a role in the treatment of high-grade (grades III and IV) gliomas in some patients.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

23411</a>

2342]</p>

2343<p id="_987" tabindex="-1">

2344Standard treatment options for primary CNS tumors include the following:</p>

2345<div class="pdq-content-list">

2346<ol id="_988">

2347<li>

2348<a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_724">

2349Surgery</a>

2350.</li>

2351<li>

2352<a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_730">

2353Radiation therapy</a>

2354.</li>

2355<li>

2356<a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_740">

2357Chemotherapy</a>

2358.</li>

2359<li>

2360<a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_974">

2361Active surveillance</a>

2362.</li>

2363<li>

2364<a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1065">

2365Supportive therapy</a>

2366.</li>

2367</ol>

2368</div>

2369<section id="_724">

2370<h4 id="_724_toc">

2371Surgery</h4>

2372<p id="_725" tabindex="-1">

2373For most types of CNS tumors in most

2374locations, complete or near-complete surgical removal is generally attempted, within the constraints of

2375preserving neurological function and the patient's underlying health. This practice is based on observational evidence that survival is better in patients who undergo tumor resection than in those who have closed biopsy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

23762</a>

2377,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

23783</a>

2379] The benefit of resection has not been tested in randomized trials. Selection bias can enter into observational studies despite attempts to adjust for patient differences that guide the decision to resect the tumor; therefore, the actual difference in outcome between radical surgery and biopsy alone may not be as large as noted in the retrospective studies.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

23803</a>

2381]</p>

2382<p id="_873" tabindex="-1">

2383 An exception to the use of resection is the case of

2384deep-seated tumors such as pontine gliomas, which are diagnosed on

2385clinical evidence and treated without initial surgery approximately 50% of the time. In most cases, however, diagnosis by biopsy is preferred.

2386Stereotactic biopsy can be used for lesions that are difficult to reach and

2387resect.

2388</p>

2389<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

2390The primary goals of surgical resection include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

23914</a>

2392]</p>

2393<div class="pdq-content-list">

2394<ul id="_1241">

2395<li>

2396To establish a histological diagnosis.</li>

2397<li>

2398To reduce intracranial pressure by removing as much tumor as is safely possible to preserve neurological function.</li>

2399</ul>

2400</div>

2401<p id="_728" tabindex="-1">

2402 Total elimination of primary malignant intraparenchymal tumors by surgery alone is rarely achievable. Therefore, intraoperative techniques have been developed to reach a balance between removing as much tumor as is practical and preserving functional status. For example, craniotomies with stereotactic resections of primary gliomas can be performed in cooperative patients while they are awake, with real-time assessment of neurological function.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

24035</a>

2404] Examples of intraoperative neurological assessment include the following:</p>

2405<div class="pdq-content-list">

2406<ul id="_1052">

2407<li>

2408 Resection proceeds until either the magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) signal abnormality being used to monitor the extent of surgery is completely removed or subtle neurological dysfunction appears (e.g., a slight decrease in rapid alternating motor movement or anomia). </li>

2409<li>

2410 When the tumor is located in or near language centers in the cortex, intraoperative language mapping can be performed by electrode discharge-induced speech arrest while the patient is asked to count or read.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

24116</a>

2412]</li>

2413</ul>

2414</div>

2415<p id="_729" tabindex="-1">

2416As is the case with several other specialized operations [<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

24177</a>

2418,<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

24198</a>

2420] in which postoperative mortality has been associated with the number of procedures performed, postoperative mortality after surgery for primary brain tumors may be associated with hospital and/or surgeon volume.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

24219</a>

2422] Using the Nationwide Inpatient Sample hospital discharge database for the years 1988 to 2000, which represented 20% of inpatient admissions to nonfederal U.S. hospitals, investigators observed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

24239</a>

2424]</p>

2425<div class="pdq-content-list">

2426<ul id="_1236">

2427<li>

2428 Large-volume hospitals had lower in-hospital mortality rates after craniotomies for primary brain tumors (odds ratio [OR], 0.75 for a tenfold higher caseload; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.62&#8211;0.90) and after needle biopsies (OR, 0.54; 95% CI, 0.35&#8211;0.83).</li>

2429<li>

2430Although there was no specific sharp threshold in all-cause mortality outcomes between low-volume hospitals and high-volume hospitals, craniotomy-associated in-hospital mortality was 4.5% for hospitals with 5 or fewer procedures per year and 1.5% for hospitals with at least 42 procedures per year.</li>

2431<li>

2432In-hospital mortality rates decreased over the study years (perhaps because the proportion of elective nonemergent operations increased from 45% to 57%), but the decrease was more rapid in high-volume hospitals than in low-volume hospitals. </li>

2433<li>

2434High-volume surgeons had lower in-hospital patient mortality rates after craniotomy (OR, 0.60; 95% CI, 0.45&#8211;0.79).</li>

2435</ul>

2436</div>

2437<p id="_1242" tabindex="-1">

2438As with any study of volume-outcome associations, these results may not be causal because of residual confounding factors such as referral patterns, private insurance, and patient selection, despite multivariable adjustment. </p>

2439</section>

2440<section id="_730">

2441<h4 id="_730_toc">

2442Radiation therapy</h4>

2443<section id="_731">

2444<h5 id="_731_toc">

2445High-grade tumors</h5>

2446<p id="_732" tabindex="-1">

2447Radiation therapy has a major role in the treatment of patients with high-grade gliomas. </p>

2448<p id="_1056" tabindex="-1">

2449Evidence (postoperative radiation therapy [PORT]):</p>

2450<div class="pdq-content-list">

2451<ol id="_1057">

2452<li>

2453A systematic review and meta-analysis of five randomized trials (plus one trial with allocation by birth date) comparing PORT with no radiation therapy showed a statistically significant survival advantage with radiation (risk ratio, 0.81; 95% CI, 0.74&#8211;0.88).[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

245410</a>

2455][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2456Level of evidence A1</a>

2457] </li>

2458<li>

2459A randomized trial comparing 60 Gy (in 30 fractions over 6 weeks) with 45 Gy (in 25 fractions over 4 weeks) showed superior survival in the first group (12 months vs. 9 months median survival; hazard ratio [HR], 0.81; 95% CI, 0.66&#8211;0.99). The accepted standard dose of EBRT for malignant gliomas is 60 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

246011</a>

2461][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2462Level of evidence A1</a>

2463]</li>

2464</ol>

2465</div>

2466<p id="_868" tabindex="-1">

2467EBRT using either 3-dimensional conformal radiation therapy (3D-CRT) or intensity-modulated radiation therapy (IMRT) is considered an acceptable technique in radiation therapy delivery. Typically used are 2- to 3-cm margins on the MRI-based volumes (T1-weighted and fluid-attenuated inversion recovery [FLAIR]) to create the planning target volume. </p>

2468<p id="_788" tabindex="-1">

2469Dose escalation using radiosurgery has not improved outcomes. A randomized trial tested radiosurgery as a boost added to standard EBRT, but the trial found no improvement in survival, quality of life, or patterns of relapse compared with EBRT without the boost.[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

247012</a>

2471,<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

247213</a>

2473] </p>

2474<p id="_735" tabindex="-1">

2475Brachytherapy has been used to deliver high doses of radiation locally to the tumor while sparing normal brain tissue. However, this approach is technically demanding and has fallen out of favor with the advent of 3D-CRT and IMRT.</p>

2476</section>

2477<section id="_736">

2478<h5 id="_736_toc">

2479Low-grade tumors</h5>

2480<p id="_737" tabindex="-1">

2481Treatment options for patients with low-grade gliomas (i.e., low-grade astrocytomas, oligodendrogliomas, and mixed oligoastrocytomas) are not as clear as in the case of high-grade tumors and include observation, PORT, and chemotherapy with temozolomide. </p>

2482<p id="_1058" tabindex="-1">

2483Evidence (PORT vs. observation):</p>

2484<div class="pdq-content-list">

2485<ol id="_1059">

2486<li>

2487The European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer (EORTC) randomly assigned 311 patients with low-grade gliomas to undergo either radiation or observation in the EORTC-22845 trial.[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

248814</a>

2489,<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

249015</a>

2491] On review of central pathology, about 25% of patients in the trial were reported to have high-grade tumors. Most of the control patients received radiation therapy at the time of progression.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2492<ul id="_1060">

2493<li>

2494After a median follow-up of 93 months, median progression-free survival (PFS) was 5.3 years in the radiation arm versus 3.4 years in the control arm (HR, 0.59; 95% CI, 0.45&#8211;0.77).[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

249514</a>

2496,<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

249715</a>

2498][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2499Level of evidence B1</a>

2500]</li>

2501<li>

2502There was no difference in the overall survival (OS). The median survival was 7.4 years in the radiation arm and 7.2 years in the control arm (HR, 0.97; 95% CI, 0.71&#8211;1.34; <em>

2503P</em>

2504 = .87).[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

250514</a>

2506,<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

250715</a>

2508][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2509Level of evidence A1</a>

2510] This was caused by a longer survival after progression in the control arm (3.4 years) than in the radiation arm (1.0 year) (<em>

2511P</em>

2512 &lt; .0001).</li>

2513<li>

2514The investigators did not collect reliable quality-of-life measurements, so it is not clear whether the delay in initial relapse in the radiation therapy arm translated into improved function or quality of life. </li>

2515</ul>

2516</div>

2517</li>

2518</ol>

2519</div>

2520<p id="_1381" tabindex="-1">

2521Evidence (PORT versus temozolomide for patients with low-grade World Health Organization (WHO) grade II tumors with at least one high-risk feature):</p>

2522<div class="pdq-content-list">

2523<ol id="_1382">

2524<li>

2525The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00182819">

2526EORTC 22033-26033</a>

2527 trial (NCT00182819) randomly assigned 707 patients with low-grade glioma (WHO grade II astrocytoma, oligoastrocytoma, or oligodendroglioma) and at least one high-risk feature (age &gt;40 years, progressive disease, tumor size &gt;5 cm, tumor crossing the midline, or neurological symptoms) to receive either radiation therapy (n = 240) or temozolomide chemotherapy (n = 237). Radiation therapy consisted of conformal treatment (up to 50.4 Gy; 28 doses of 1.8 Gy daily, 5 days a week, for up to 6.5 weeks). Chemotherapy was dose-dense oral temozolomide (75 mg/m<span class="sup">

25282</span>

2529 daily for 21 days, repeated every 28 days [one cycle], for a maximum of 12 cycles).[<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

253016</a>

2531,<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

253217</a>

2533]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2534<ol id="_1389" class="lower-alpha">

2535<li>

2536There was no significant difference in PFS (primary end point) or health-related quality of life (secondary end point).</li>

2537<li>

2538At a median follow-up of 48 months (intraquartile range, 31&#8211;56), median PFS was 39 months (95% CI, 35&#8211;44) in the temozolomide group and 46 months (95% CI, 40&#8211;56) in the radiation therapy group (unadjusted HR, 1.16; 95% CI, 0.9&#8211;1.5; <em>

2539P</em>

2540 = .22).[<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

254116</a>

2542][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2543Level of evidence B1</a>

2544] </li>

2545<li>

2546An exploratory analysis of 318 molecularly defined patients found that patients with an <em class="gene-name">

2547IDH</em>

2548 gene mutation without codeletion of 1p/19q displayed a significantly longer PFS when treated with radiation therapy (HR, 1.86; 95% CI, 1.21&#8211;2.87; log-rank, <em>

2549P</em>

2550 = .0043).</li>

2551<li>

2552There were no significant treatment-dependent differences in PFS for patients with <em class="gene-name">

2553IDH</em>

2554 mutation with codeletion of 1p/19q and <em class="gene-name">

2555IDH</em>

2556 wild-type tumors. </li>

2557<li>

2558Patients with wild-type <em class="gene-name">

2559IDH</em>

2560 tumors had the worst prognosis independent of treatment type.</li>

2561<li>

2562Patients with <em class="gene-name">

2563IDH</em>

2564-mutated tumors with codeletion of 1p/19q had the best prognosis.</li>

2565<li>

2566The O6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase (MGMT) promoter status was methylated in the following: <div class="pdq-content-list">

2567<ul id="_1390">

2568<li>

2569All <em class="gene-name">

2570IDH</em>

2571 mutations with codeletion of 1p/19q (45/45).</li>

2572<li>

2573Sixty-two of 72 (86%) of the <em class="gene-name">

2574IDH</em>

2575 mutations without codeletion of 1p/19q.</li>

2576<li>

2577Five of nine (56%) of the <em class="gene-name">

2578IDH</em>

2579 wild-type cases. </li>

2580</ul>

2581</div>

2582</li>

2583</ol>

2584</div>

2585</li>

2586</ol>

2587</div>

2588</section>

2589<section id="_738">

2590<h5 id="_738_toc">

2591Disease progression, subsequent neoplasms, or recurrences</h5>

2592<p id="_739" tabindex="-1">

2593There are no randomized trials to delineate the role of repeat radiation after disease progression or the development of radiation-induced cancers. The literature is limited to small retrospective case series, which makes interpretation difficult.[<a href="#cit/section_3.18">

259418</a>

2595] The decision to repeat radiation must be made carefully because of the risk of neurocognitive deficits and radiation-induced necrosis. One advantage of radiosurgery is the ability to deliver therapeutic doses to recurrent tumors that may require the re-irradiation of previously irradiated brain tissue beyond tolerable dose limits.</p>

2596</section>

2597</section>

2598<section id="_740">

2599<h4 id="_740_toc">

2600Chemotherapy</h4>

2601<section id="_741">

2602<h5 id="_741_toc">

2603Systemic chemotherapy</h5>

2604<p id="_780" tabindex="-1">

2605For many years, the nitrosourea carmustine ([bis-chloroethylnitrosourea] BCNU) was the standard chemotherapy agent added to surgery and radiation therapy for malignant gliomas, based on the Radiation Therapy Oncology Group's (RTOG's) randomized trial (RTOG-8302).[<a href="#cit/section_3.19">

260619</a>

2607][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2608Level of evidence A1</a>

2609] A modest impact on survival with the use of nitrosourea-containing chemotherapy regimens for malignant gliomas was confirmed in a patient-level meta-analysis of 12 randomized trials (combined HR<sub>

2610death</sub>

2611, 0.85; 95% CI, 0.78&#8211;0.91).[<a href="#cit/section_3.20">

261220</a>

2613] </p>

2614<p id="_743" tabindex="-1">

2615A large multicenter trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006353">

2616NCT00006353</a>

2617) of glioblastoma patients conducted by the EORTC-National Cancer Institute of Canada reported a survival advantage with the use of temozolomide in addition to radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.21">

261821</a>

2619,<a href="#cit/section_3.22">

262022</a>

2621][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2622Level of evidence A1</a>

2623] On the basis of these results, the oral agent temozolomide has replaced BCNU as the standard systemic chemotherapy for malignant gliomas. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1100">

2624Glioblastomas treatment</a>

2625 section.</p>

2626<p id="_1348" tabindex="-1">

2627Long-term results of randomized trials in high-risk, low-grade (WHO grade II) gliomas [<a href="#cit/section_3.23">

262823</a>

2629][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2630Level of evidence A1</a>

2631] and anaplastic (WHO grade III) oligodendroglial tumors [<a href="#cit/section_3.24">

263224</a>

2633,<a href="#cit/section_3.25">

263425</a>

2635][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2636Level of evidence A1</a>

2637] have demonstrated that the addition of procarbazine, lomustine, and vincristine (PCV) chemotherapy to radiation therapy after surgery extends survival. Radiation and PCV chemotherapy should be considered for patients deemed appropriate for therapy. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_792">

2638Treatment of Primary Central Nervous System Tumors by Tumor Type</a>

2639 section.</p>

2640<section id="_744">

2641<h6 id="_744_toc">

2642Localized chemotherapy (carmustine wafer)</h6>

2643<p id="_745" tabindex="-1">

2644The concept of delivering high doses of chemotherapy while avoiding systemic toxicity is attractive because malignant glioma&#8211;related deaths are nearly always the result of an inability to control intracranial disease rather than the result of distant metastases. A biodegradable carmustine wafer has been developed for that purpose. The wafers contain 3.85% carmustine, and up to eight wafers are implanted into the tumor bed lining at the time of open resection, with an intended total dose of about 7.7 mg per wafer (61.6 mg maximum per patient) over a period of 2 to 3 weeks. </p>

2645<p id="_1243" tabindex="-1">

2646Two randomized placebo-controlled trials of this focal drug-delivery method have shown an OS advantage associated with the carmustine wafers versus radiation therapy alone. In both trials, the upper age limit for patients was 65 years. </p>

2647<p id="_1061" tabindex="-1">

2648Evidence (carmustine wafer):</p>

2649<div class="pdq-content-list">

2650<ol id="_1062">

2651<li>

2652A small trial was closed because of a lack of continued availability of the carmustine wafers after 32 patients with high-grade gliomas had been entered.[<a href="#cit/section_3.26">

265326</a>

2654]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2655<ul id="_1063">

2656<li>

2657Although OS was better in the carmustine-wafer group (median 58.1 vs. 39.9 weeks; <em>

2658P</em>

2659 = .012), there was an imbalance in the study arms (only 11 of 16 patients in the carmustine-wafer group vs. 16 of the 16 patients in the placebo-wafer group had grade IV glioblastoma tumors).</li>

2660</ul>

2661</div>

2662</li>

2663<li>

2664A multicenter study of 240 patients with primary malignant gliomas, 207 of whom had glioblastoma, was more informative.[<a href="#cit/section_3.27">

266527</a>

2666,<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

266728</a>

2668] At initial surgery, patients received either carmustine wafers or placebo wafers, followed by radiation therapy (55&#8211;60 Gy). Systemic therapy was not allowed until recurrence, except in the case of anaplastic oligodendrogliomas (n = 9). Unlike the initial trial, patient characteristics were well balanced between the study arms.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2669<ul id="_1064">

2670<li>

2671 Median survival in the two groups was 13.8 months in patients treated with carmustine wafers versus 11.6 months in placebo-treated patients (HR, 0.73; 95% CI, 0.56&#8211;0.96; <em>

2672P</em>

2673 = .017).</li>

2674</ul>

2675</div>

2676</li>

2677<li>

2678A systematic review combining both studies [<a href="#cit/section_3.26">

267926</a>

2680-<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

268128</a>

2682] estimated an HR for overall mortality of 0.65; 95% CI, 0.48&#8211;0.86; <em>

2683P</em>

2684 = .003.[<a href="#cit/section_3.29">

268529</a>

2686][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2687Level of evidence A1</a>

2688]</li>

2689</ol>

2690</div>

2691</section>

2692</section>

2693</section>

2694<section id="_974">

2695<h4 id="_974_toc">

2696Active surveillance</h4>

2697<p id="_1055" tabindex="-1">

2698Active surveillance is appropriate in some circumstances. With the increasing use of sensitive neuroimaging tools, detection of asymptomatic low-grade meningiomas has increased; most appear to show minimal growth and can often be safely observed, with therapy deferred until the detection of tumor growth or the development of symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_3.30">

269930</a>

2700,<a href="#cit/section_3.31">

270131</a>

2702]</p>

2703</section>

2704<section id="_1065">

2705<h4 id="_1065_toc">

2706Supportive therapy</h4>

2707<p id="_1066" tabindex="-1">

2708Dexamethasone, mannitol, and furosemide are used to treat the peritumoral edema associated with brain tumors. The use of anticonvulsants is mandatory for patients with seizures.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

27094</a>

2710]</p>

2711</section>

2712</section>

3003</td>

3004</tr>
