3209<section id="_1067">

3210<h3 id="_1067_toc">

3211Astrocytic Tumors Treatment</h3>

3212<section id="_1068">

3213<h4 id="_1068_toc">

3214Brain stem gliomas treatment</h4>

3215<p id="_1244" tabindex="-1">

3216Patients with brain stem gliomas have relatively poor prognoses that correlate with

3217histology (when biopsies are performed), location, and extent of tumor. The

3218overall median survival time of patients in studies has been 44 to 74

3219weeks.</p>

3220<p id="_1245" tabindex="-1">

3221Standard treatment options for brain stem gliomas include the following:</p>

3222<div class="pdq-content-list">

3223<ol id="_1336">

3224<li>

3225Radiation therapy.</li>

3226</ol>

3227</div>

3228</section>

3229<section id="_1073">

3230<h4 id="_1073_toc">

3231Pineal astrocytic tumors treatment</h4>

3232<p id="_1247" tabindex="-1">

3233Depending on the degree of anaplasia, patients with pineal astrocytomas have variable prognoses.

3234Patients with higher-grade tumors have worse prognoses.

3235</p>

3236<p id="_1248" tabindex="-1">

3237Standard treatment options for pineal astrocytic tumors include the following:</p>

3238<div class="pdq-content-list">

3239<ol id="_1249">

3240<li>

3241Surgery plus radiation therapy for pineal astrocytoma.</li>

3242<li>

3243Surgery plus radiation therapy and chemotherapy for higher-grade tumors.</li>

3244</ol>

3245</div>

3246</section>

3247<section id="_1078">

3248<h4 id="_1078_toc">

3249Pilocytic astrocytomas treatment</h4>

3250<p id="_1250" tabindex="-1">

3251This astrocytic tumor is classified as a World Health Organization (WHO) grade I tumor and is often curable.</p>

3252<p id="_1251" tabindex="-1">

3253Standard treatment options for pilocytic astrocytomas include the following:</p>

3254<div class="pdq-content-list">

3255<ol id="_1252">

3256<li>

3257Surgery alone if the tumor is totally resectable.

3258</li>

3259<li>

3260Surgery followed by radiation therapy to known or suspected residual

3261tumor.</li>

3262</ol>

3263</div>

3264</section>

3265<section id="_1083">

3266<h4 id="_1083_toc">

3267Diffuse astrocytomas treatment</h4>

3268<p id="_1253" tabindex="-1">

3269This WHO grade II astrocytic tumor is less often curable than is a pilocytic astrocytoma.</p>

3270<p id="_1254" tabindex="-1">

3271Standard treatment options for diffuse astrocytomas (WHO grade II) include the following:</p>

3272<div class="pdq-content-list">

3273<ol id="_1255">

3274<li>

3275Surgery with or without radiation therapy.</li>

3276<li>

3277Surgery followed by radiation therapy and chemotherapy.</li>

3278</ol>

3279</div>

3280<p id="_1368" tabindex="-1">

3281Controversy exists about the timing of radiation therapy after surgery. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_736">

3282Low-grade tumors</a>

3283 section.</p>

3284<div class="pdq-content-list">

3285<ul id="_1369">

3286<li>

3287Radiation therapy improved progression-free survival (PFS) in patients who received early radiation therapy in the European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer (EORTC) EORTC-22845 trial. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1138">

3288Oligodendrogliomas treatment</a>

3289 section.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

32901</a>

3291][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3292Level of evidence A1</a>

3293] </li>

3294<li>

3295In the same trial, there was no difference in overall survival (OS) between patients who had radiation therapy after surgery and those who were treated with radiation therapy at the time of progression.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

32961</a>

3297][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3298Level of evidence A1</a>

3299]</li>

3300</ul>

3301</div>

3302<p id="_1370" tabindex="-1">

3303Some

3304physicians use surgery alone if a patient has clinical factors that are considered low risk, such as age younger than 40 years and the lack of contrast enhancement on a computed

3305tomographic scan.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

33062</a>

3307]</p>

3308<p id="_1351" tabindex="-1">

3309Evidence (surgery followed by radiation therapy and chemotherapy):</p>

3310<div class="pdq-content-list">

3311<ol id="_1352">

3312<li>

3313For patients with low-grade (WHO grade II) tumors, which are considered <em>

3314high risk</em>

3315, radiation therapy followed by six cycles of vincristine (PCV) chemotherapy is a recommended option based on the long-term follow-up results of the Radiation Therapy Oncology Group's (RTOG's) 1986-initiated randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003375">

3316RTOG 9802</a>

3317 [NCT00003375]).[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

33183</a>

3319][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3320Level of evidence A1</a>

3321] In this trial, high-risk, low-grade glioma patients, defined as patients aged 18 to 39 years with biopsy or subtotal resection, or patients aged 40 years or older, were randomly assigned to either 54 Gy of radiation therapy or radiation therapy followed by six cycles of PCV chemotherapy.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3322<ol id="_1361" class="lower-alpha">

3323<li>

3324The addition of PCV to radiation therapy increased median PFS from 4.0 years to 10.4 years (<em>

3325P</em>

3326 = .002; hazard ratio [HR], 0.50) and median OS from 7.8 years to 13.3 years (<em>

3327P</em>

3328 = .03; HR, 0.59).</li>

3329<li>

3330Notably, the RTOG 9802 study enrolled patients with a variety of tumors, including astrocytomas, oligodendrogliomas, and mixed oligoastrocytomas.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3331<ul id="_1362">

3332<li>

3333 In a risk-adjusted multivariate analysis, patients treated with PCV and patients with an oligodendroglial histology had better survival outcomes. A subset analysis of histological type suggested that the addition of PCV mainly benefited patients with oligodendroglial tumors, although this data is yet to be validated.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

33344</a>

3335]</li>

3336<li>

3337 Median OS for PCV versus the control arm was not reached versus 10.8 years for oligodendrogliomas (<em>

3338P</em>

3339 = .008), 11.4 years versus 5.9 years for oligoastrocytomas (<em>

3340P</em>

3341 = .05), and 7.7 years versus 4.4 years for astrocytomas (<em>

3342P</em>

3343 = .31).</li>

3344</ul>

3345</div>

3346</li>

3347</ol>

3348</div>

3349</li>

3350</ol>

3351</div>

3352<p id="_1371" tabindex="-1">

3353The discovery of the <em class="gene-name">

3354IDH1</em>

3355 and <em class="gene-name">

3356IDH2</em>

3357 mutations in diffuse gliomas has greatly helped to identify patients who are considered <em>

3358high risk</em>

3359. A number of large, retrospective studies has demonstrated that the <em class="gene-name">

3360IDH1</em>

3361 and <em class="gene-name">

3362IDH2</em>

3363 mutations are a powerful independent prognostic factor for improved survival.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

33645</a>

3365-<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

33669</a>

3367] Most WHO grade II and III gliomas harbor the <em class="gene-name">

3368IDH1</em>

3369 and <em class="gene-name">

3370IDH2</em>

3371 mutations,[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

33726</a>

3373,<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

337410</a>

3375,<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

337611</a>

3377] and, therefore, the presence of the <em class="gene-name">

3378IDH1</em>

3379 and <em class="gene-name">

3380IDH2</em>

3381 mutations should be included in the assessment of <em>

3382high risk</em>

3383. Molecular correlative data from the RTOG 98-02 trial, which would be informative about which patients benefited the most from the addition of PCV, have not yet been reported.</p>

3384</section>

3385<section id="_1089">

3386<h4 id="_1089_toc">

3387Anaplastic astrocytomas treatment</h4>

3388<p id="_1257" tabindex="-1">

3389Patients with anaplastic astrocytomas (WHO grade III) have a low cure rate with standard

3390local treatment.</p>

3391<p id="_1258" tabindex="-1">

3392Standard treatment options for anaplastic astrocytomas include the following:</p>

3393<div class="pdq-content-list">

3394<ol id="_1259">

3395<li>

3396Surgery plus radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy.</li>

3397<li>

3398Surgery plus chemotherapy.</li>

3399</ol>

3400</div>

3401<p id="_1260" tabindex="-1">

3402A subset of anaplastic astrocytomas is aggressive; these tumors are frequently managed in the same way as glioblastomas, with surgery and radiation, and often with chemotherapy. However, the optimal treatment for these tumors is not established. Two phase III randomized trials restricted to patients with anaplastic gliomas (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00626990">

3403NCT00626990</a>

3404 and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00887146">

3405NCT00887146</a>

3406) are currently enrolling patients, but efficacy data are not available. It is not known whether the improved survival of patients with chemotherapy-treated glioblastoma can be extrapolated to patients with anaplastic astrocytomas. </p>

3407<p id="_1372" tabindex="-1">

3408The <em class="gene-name">

3409IDH1</em>

3410 and <em class="gene-name">

3411IDH2</em>

3412 mutations are present in 50% to 70% of anaplastic astrocytomas and are independently associated with significantly improved survival.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

34136</a>

3414,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

34159</a>

3416] Assessment of the <em class="gene-name">

3417IDH1</em>

3418 and <em class="gene-name">

3419IDH2</em>

3420 mutation status may guide decisions about treatment options.</p>

3421<p id="_1261" tabindex="-1">

3422Evidence (surgery plus radiation therapy or chemotherapy):</p>

3423<div class="pdq-content-list">

3424<ol id="_1262">

3425<li>

3426Postoperative radiation alone has been compared with postoperative chemotherapy alone in patients with anaplastic gliomas (i.e., 144 astrocytomas, 91 oligoastrocytomas, and 39 oligodendrogliomas), with crossover to the other modality at the time of tumor progression. Of the 139 patients randomly assigned to undergo radiation therapy, 135 were randomly assigned to receive chemotherapy, with a 32-week course of either PCV or single-agent temozolomide (2:1:1 randomization).[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

342712</a>

3428][Levels of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3429A1</a>

3430 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3431B1</a>

3432] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3433<ul id="_1263">

3434<li>

3435The order of the modalities did not affect time-to-treatment failure (TTF) or OS.</li>

3436<li>

3437Neither TTF nor OS differed across the treatment arms.</li>

3438</ul>

3439</div>

3440</li>

3441</ol>

3442</div>

3443<p id="_1264" tabindex="-1">

3444Patients with anaplastic astrocytomas are appropriate candidates for clinical

3445trials designed to improve local control by adding newer forms of treatment to

3446standard treatment. Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3447NCI website</a>

3448.</p>

3449</section>

3450<section id="_1100">

3451<h4 id="_1100_toc">

3452Glioblastomas treatment</h4>

3453<p id="_1265" tabindex="-1">

3454For patients with glioblastoma (WHO grade IV), the cure rate is very low with standard local

3455treatment.</p>

3456<p id="_1354" tabindex="-1">

3457Methylation of the promoter of the <em class="gene-name">

3458MGMT</em>

3459 DNA repair enzyme gene is an independent prognostic factor for improved survival in newly diagnosed glioblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

346013</a>

3461,<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

346214</a>

3463] <em class="gene-name">

3464MGMT</em>

3465 promoter methylation and concomitant inactivation of the DNA repair enzyme activities may also predict for response to temozolomide chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

346613</a>

3467] However, the clinical data that <em class="gene-name">

3468MGMT</em>

3469 promoter methylation is a predictive marker is less certain. </p>

3470<p id="_1266" tabindex="-1">

3471Standard treatment options for patients with newly diagnosed glioblastoma include the following:</p>

3472<div class="pdq-content-list">

3473<ol id="_1267">

3474<li>

3475Surgery plus radiation therapy and chemotherapy.</li>

3476<li>

3477Surgery plus radiation therapy.</li>

3478<li>

3479Carmustine-impregnated polymer implanted during initial surgery.</li>

3480<li>

3481Radiation therapy and concurrent chemotherapy.</li>

3482</ol>

3483</div>

3484<p id="_1268" tabindex="-1">

3485The standard treatment for patients with newly diagnosed glioblastoma is surgery followed by concurrent radiation therapy and daily temozolomide, and then followed by six cycles of temozolomide. The addition of bevacizumab to radiation therapy and temozolomide did not improve OS.</p>

3486<p id="_1270" tabindex="-1">

3487Evidence (surgery plus radiation therapy and chemotherapy):</p>

3488<div class="pdq-content-list">

3489<ol id="_1271">

3490<li>

3491Standard therapy is based on a large, multicenter, randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006353">

3492NCT00006353</a>

3493) conducted by the EORTC and National Cancer Institute of Canada (NCIC).This trial reported a survival benefit with concurrent radiation therapy and temozolomide, compared with radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

349415</a>

3495,<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

349616</a>

3497][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3498Level of evidence A1</a>

3499] In this study, 573 patients with glioblastoma were randomly assigned to receive standard radiation to the tumor volume with a 2- to 3-cm margin (60 Gy, 2 Gy per fraction, over 6 weeks) alone or with temozolomide (75 mg/m<span class="sup">

35002</span>

3501 orally per day during radiation therapy for up to 49 days, followed by a 4-week break and then up to six cycles of five daily doses every 28 days at a dose of 150 mg/m<span class="sup">

35022</span>

3503, increasing to 200 mg/m<span class="sup">

35042</span>

3505 after the first cycle). <div class="pdq-content-list">

3506<ol id="_1272" class="lower-alpha">

3507<li>

3508OS was statistically significantly better in the combined radiation therapy&#8211;temozolomide group (HR<sub>

3509death</sub>

3510, 0.6; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.5&#8211;0.7; OS rate at 3 years was 16.0% for the radiation therapy&#8211;temozolomide group vs. 4.4% in the radiation therapy&#8211;alone group).</li>

3511<li>

3512A companion molecular correlation subset study to the EORTC-NCIC trial provided strong evidence that epigenetic silencing of the MGMT DNA-repair gene by promoter DNA methylation was associated with increased OS in patients with newly diagnosed glioblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

351313</a>

3514]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3515<ul id="_1273">

3516<li>

3517MGMT promoter methylation was an independent favorable prognostic factor (<em>

3518P</em>

3519 &lt; .001 by the log-rank test; HR, 0.45; 95% CI, 0.32&#8211;0.61). </li>

3520<li>

3521The median OS for patients with <em class="gene-name">

3522MGMT</em>

3523 methylation was 18.2 months (95% CI, 15.5&#8211;22.0), compared with 12.2 months (95% CI, 11.4&#8211;13.5) for patients without <em class="gene-name">

3524MGMT</em>

3525 methylation.</li>

3526</ul>

3527</div>

3528</li>

3529</ol>

3530</div>

3531</li>

3532<li>

3533To test whether protracted (dose-dense) temozolomide enhances treatment response in patients with newly diagnosed glioblastoma, a multicenter, randomized, phase III trial conducted by the RTOG, EORTC, and the North Central Cancer Therapy Group, <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00304031">

3534RTOG 0525</a>

3535 (NCT00304031), compared standard adjuvant temozolomide treatment (days 1&#8211;5 of a 28-day cycle) with a dose-dense schedule (days 1&#8211;21 of a 28-day cycle). All patients were treated with surgery followed by radiation therapy and concurrent daily temozolomide. Patients were then randomly assigned to receive either standard adjuvant temozolomide or dose-dense temozolomide.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

353614</a>

3537][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3538Level of evidence A1</a>

3539]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3540<ul id="_1274">

3541<li>

3542Among 833 randomly assigned patients, no statistically significant difference between standard and dose-dense temozolomide was observed for median OS (16.6 months for standard temozolomide vs. 14.9 months for dose-dense temozolomide; HR, 1.03; <em>

3543P</em>

3544 = .63) or for median PFS (5.5 vs. 6.7 months; HR, 0.87; <em>

3545P</em>

3546 = .06). </li>

3547<li>

3548 Protracted temozolomide, which depletes intracellular MGMT, was predicted to have greater efficacy in tumors with <em class="gene-name">

3549MGMT</em>

3550-promoter methylation. To test this retrospectively, MGMT status was determined in 86% of randomly assigned patients. No difference in efficacy was observed in either the <em class="gene-name">

3551MGMT</em>

3552-methylated or <em class="gene-name">

3553MGMT</em>

3554-unmethylated subsets. There was no survival advantage for the use of dose-dense temozolomide versus standard-dose temozolomide in newly diagnosed glioblastoma patients, regardless of <em class="gene-name">

3555MGMT</em>

3556 status. However, this study confirmed the strong prognostic effect of <em class="gene-name">

3557MGMT</em>

3558 methylation because the median OS was 21.2 months (95% CI, 17.9&#8211;24.8) for patients with methylation versus 14 months (HR, 1.74; 95% CI, 12.9&#8211;14.7; <em>

3559P</em>

3560 &lt; .001) for patients without methylation. </li>

3561<li>

3562The efficacy of dose-dense temozolomide for patients who have recurrent glioblastoma, however, is yet to be determined.</li>

3563</ul>

3564</div>

3565</li>

3566</ol>

3567</div>

3568<p id="_1275" tabindex="-1">

3569Evidence (surgery and chemoradiation with or without bevacizumab):</p>

3570<p id="_1276" tabindex="-1">

3571In 2013, final data from two multicenter, phase III, randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled trials of bevacizumab in patients who had newly diagnosed glioblastoma were reported: <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00884741">

3572RTOG 0825</a>

3573 (NCT00884741) and the Roche-sponsored <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00943826">

3574AVAglio</a>

3575 (NCT00943826).[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

357617</a>

3577,<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

357818</a>

3579][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3580Level of evidence A1</a>

3581] Bevacizumab did not improve OS in either trial. </p>

3582<p id="_1277" tabindex="-1">

3583There was significant crossover in both trials. Approximately 40% of RTOG 0825 patients and approximately 30% of AVAglio patients received bevacizumab at the first sign of disease progression.</p>

3584<div class="pdq-content-list">

3585<ol id="_1278">

3586<li>

3587<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00884741">

3588RTOG 0825</a>

3589 (NCT00884741): Patients were randomly assigned to receive standard therapy (chemoradiation therapy with temozolomide) or standard therapy plus bevacizumab. OS and PFS were coprimary end points.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

359017</a>

3591][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3592Level of evidence A1</a>

3593]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3594<ul id="_1279">

3595<li>

3596Bevacizumab did not improve OS (median OS was 16&#8211;17 months for each arm); however, it increased median PFS (10.7 months in the bevacizumab arm vs. 7.3 months in the placebo arm; HR, 0.79; <em>

3597P</em>

3598 = .007).</li>

3599<li>

3600The PFS result in the RTOG 0825 trial did not meet the prespecified significance level (<em>

3601P</em>

3602 = .004). </li>

3603</ul>

3604</div>

3605 </li>

3606<li>

3607<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00943826">

3608AVAglio</a>

3609 (NCT00943826): Patients were randomly assigned to receive standard therapy (chemoradiation with temozolomide) or standard therapy plus bevacizumab. OS and PFS were coprimary end points.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

361018</a>

3611][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3612Level of evidence A1</a>

3613] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3614<ul id="_1280">

3615<li>

3616Bevacizumab did not improve OS (median OS was 16&#8211;17 months for each arm); however, it increased median PFS (10.6 months in the bevacizumab arm vs. 6.2 months in the placebo arm; HR, 0.64; <em>

3617P</em>

3618 &lt; .0001).</li>

3619<li>

3620The PFS result was statistically significant and associated with clinical benefit because bevacizumab-treated patients remained functionally independent longer (9.0 months in the bevacizumab arm vs. 6.0 months in the standard therapy arm) and had a longer time until their Karnofsky Performance status deteriorated (HR, 0.65; <em>

3621P</em>

3622 &lt; .0001). </li>

3623<li>

3624Bevacizumab-treated patients also had delayed initiation of corticosteroids (12.3 months vs. 3.7 months; HR, 0.71; <em>

3625P</em>

3626 = .002), and more patients were able to discontinue corticosteroids if they were already taking them (66% in the bevacizumab arm vs. 47% in the standard therapy arm). </li>

3627</ul>

3628</div>

3629</li>

3630</ol>

3631</div>

3632<p id="_1281" tabindex="-1">

3633The two trials had contradictory results in health-related quality of life (HRQoL) and neurocognitive outcomes studies. In the mandatory HRQoL studies in the AVAglio trial, bevacizumab-treated patients experienced improved HRQoL, but bevacizumab-treated patients in the elective RTOG 0825 studies showed more decline in patient-reported HRQoL and neurocognitive function. The reasons for these discrepancies are unclear.</p>

3634<p id="_1282" tabindex="-1">

3635On the basis of these results, there is no definite evidence that the addition of bevacizumab to standard therapy is beneficial for all newly diagnosed glioblastoma patients. Certain subgroups may benefit from the addition of bevacizumab, but this is not yet known.</p>

3636<p id="_1283" tabindex="-1">

3637Glioblastoma patients are appropriate candidates for clinical trials

3638designed to improve local control by adding newer forms of treatment to

3639standard treatment.

3640Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3641NCI website</a>

3642.</p>

3643</section>

3644</section>

3645<section id="_1137">

3646<h3 id="_1137_toc">

3647Oligodendroglial Tumors Treatment</h3>

3648<section id="_1138">

3649<h4 id="_1138_toc">

3650Oligodendrogliomas treatment</h4>

3651<p id="_1284" tabindex="-1">

3652Patients who have oligodendrogliomas (WHO grade II) generally have better prognoses than do patients who have diffuse astrocytomas. In particular, patients who have oligodendrogliomas with 1p/19q codeletion have a much longer survival.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

36533</a>

3654] Most of the oligodendrogliomas eventually progress.</p>

3655<p id="_1285" tabindex="-1">

3656Standard treatment options for oligodendrogliomas include the following:</p>

3657<div class="pdq-content-list">

3658<ol id="_1286">

3659<li>

3660Surgery with or without radiation therapy.</li>

3661<li>

3662Surgery with radiation therapy and chemotherapy.</li>

3663</ol>

3664</div>

3665<p id="_1287" tabindex="-1">

3666Controversy exists concerning the timing of radiation therapy after surgery. A study (EORTC-22845) of 300 patients with low-grade gliomas who had surgery and were randomly assigned to either radiation therapy or watchful waiting, did not show a difference in OS between the two groups.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

36671</a>

3668][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3669Level of evidence A1</a>

3670] For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_736">

3671Low-grade tumors</a>

3672 section.</p>

3673<p id="_1355" tabindex="-1">

3674For low-grade (WHO grade II) tumors that are considered <em>

3675high risk</em>

3676, radiation therapy followed by six cycles of PCV chemotherapy is a recommended option based on the long-term follow-up results of RTOG-9802, a randomized trial for high-risk, low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

36773</a>

3678][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3679Level of evidence A1</a>

3680] Notably, RTOG-9802 enrolled patients with a mixed variety of tumors, including astrocytomas, oligodendrogliomas, and mixed oligoastrocytomas. In a retrospective subset analysis, only the oligodendroglial tumors appeared to benefit from the addition of PCV.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

36814</a>

3682]. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1083">

3683Diffuse astrocytomas treatment</a>

3684 section.</p>

3685<p id="_1357" tabindex="-1">

3686The discovery of the <em class="gene-name">

3687IDH1</em>

3688 and <em class="gene-name">

3689IDH2</em>

3690 mutations, which are independent prognostic factors for significantly improved survival in diffuse gliomas, has greatly helped to identify patients who are considered <em>

3691high risk</em>

3692. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1083">

3693Diffuse astrocytomas treatment</a>

3694 section. In addition, a high proportion of WHO grade II oligodendrogliomas have 1p/19q codeletion, which is a powerful prognostic factor for improved survival.[<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

369519</a>

3696-<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

369721</a>

3698] Therefore, the presence of the <em class="gene-name">

3699IDH1</em>

3700 and <em class="gene-name">

3701IDH2</em>

3702 mutations and 1p/19q codeletion should be included in the assessment of high-risk patients. Molecular correlative data from the RTOG-9802 trial, which would be informative about which patients benefited most from the addition of PCV, have not yet been reported.</p>

3703</section>

3704<section id="_1144">

3705<h4 id="_1144_toc">

3706Anaplastic oligodendrogliomas treatment</h4>

3707<p id="_1288" tabindex="-1">

3708Patients with anaplastic oligodendrogliomas (WHO grade III) have a low cure rate with

3709standard local treatment, but their prognoses are generally better than are the prognoses of patients with anaplastic astrocytomas. Prognoses are particularly better for patients with 1p/19q codeletion, which occurs in most of these tumors. Two phase III randomized trials restricted to patients with anaplastic gliomas (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00626990">

3710NCT00626990</a>

3711 and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00887146">

3712NCT00887146</a>

3713) are currently enrolling patients; however, efficacy data are not yet available. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1089">

3714Anaplastic astrocytomas treatment</a>

3715 section. These patients are appropriate candidates for

3716clinical trials designed to improve local control by adding newer forms of

3717treatment. </p>

3718<p id="_1289" tabindex="-1">

3719Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3720NCI website</a>

3721.</p>

3722<p id="_1290" tabindex="-1">

3723Standard treatment options for anaplastic oligodendrogliomas include the following:</p>

3724<div class="pdq-content-list">

3725<ol id="_1291">

3726<li>

3727Surgery plus radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

372822</a>

3729]</li>

3730</ol>

3731</div>

3732<p id="_1292" tabindex="-1">

3733Evidence (surgery followed by radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy):</p>

3734<div class="pdq-content-list">

3735<ol id="_1293">

3736<li>

3737Mature results from the EORTC <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002840">

3738Brain Tumor Group Study 26951</a>

3739 (NCT00002840), a phase III randomized study with 11.7 years of follow-up, demonstrated increased OS and PFS in patients with anaplastic oligodendroglial tumors with six cycles of adjuvant PCV chemotherapy after radiation therapy, compared with radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

374023</a>

3741][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3742Level of evidence A1</a>

3743]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3744<ul id="_1294">

3745<li>

3746OS was significantly longer in the radiation therapy and PCV arm (42.3 months vs. 30.6 months; HR, 0.75; 95% CI, 0.60&#8211;0.95). </li>

3747<li>

3748Patients with 1p/19q-codeleted tumors derived more benefit from adjuvant PCV chemotherapy than did those with non&#8211;1p/19q-deleted tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

374923</a>

3750]</li>

3751</ul>

3752</div>

3753</li>

3754<li>

3755In contrast, the RTOG trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002569">

3756RTOG-9402</a>

3757 [NCT00002569]) demonstrated no differences in median survival by treatment arm between an 8-week, intensive PCV chemotherapy regimen followed by immediate involved-field-plus-radiation therapy and radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

375824</a>

3759]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3760<ul id="_1295">

3761<li>

3762In an unplanned subgroup analysis, patients with 1p/19q-codeleted anaplastic oligodendrogliomas and mixed anaplastic astrocytomas demonstrated a median survival of 14.7 years versus 7.3 years (HR, 0.59; 95% CI, 0.37&#8211;0.95; <em>

3763P</em>

3764 = .03).</li>

3765<li>

3766For patients with non-codeleted tumors, there was no difference in median survival by treatment arm (2.6 vs. 2.7 years; HR, 0.85; 95% CI, 0.58&#8211;1.23; <em>

3767P</em>

3768 = .39).[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

376924</a>

3770][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3771Level of evidence A1</a>

3772]</li>

3773</ul>

3774</div>

3775</li>

3776<li>

3777Postoperative radiation therapy alone has been compared with postoperative chemotherapy alone in patients with anaplastic gliomas (including 144 astrocytomas, 91 oligoastrocytomas, and 39 oligodendrogliomas) with crossover to the other modality at the time of tumor progression. Of the 139 patients randomly assigned to undergo radiation therapy, 135 were randomly assigned to receive chemotherapy, with a 32-week course of either PCV or single-agent temozolomide (2:1:1 randomization).[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

377812</a>

3779][Levels of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3780A1</a>

3781 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3782B1</a>

3783]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3784<ul id="_1296">

3785<li>

3786TTF or OS did not differ across the treatment arms and were not affected by the order of the modalities.</li>

3787</ul>

3788</div>

3789 </li>

3790</ol>

3791</div>

3792<p id="_1297" tabindex="-1">

3793On the basis of these data, <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00887146">

3794CODEL</a>

3795 (NCT00887146), a study that randomly assigned patients to receive radiation therapy alone (control arm), radiation therapy with temozolomide, and temozolomide alone (exploratory arm), was halted because radiation therapy alone was no longer considered adequate treatment in patients with anaplastic oligodendroglioma with 1p/19q-codeletions.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

379625</a>

3797] Temozolomide and PCV chemotherapy in anaplastic oligodendroglioma have not been compared, although in the setting of grade 3 anaplastic gliomas, no survival difference was seen between PCV chemotherapy and temozolomide.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

379812</a>

3799,<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

380026</a>

3801]</p>

3802<p id="_1298" tabindex="-1">

3803The combination of radiation and chemotherapy is not known to be superior in outcome to sequential modality therapy.</p>

3804<p id="_1358" tabindex="-1">

3805A high proportion of anaplastic oligodendrogliomas have the <em class="gene-name">

3806IDH1</em>

3807 and<em class="gene-name">

3808IDH2</em>

3809 mutations and 1p/19q codeletion, both powerful prognostic factors for improved survival. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1083">

3810Diffuse astrocytomas treatment</a>

3811 section.[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

381223</a>

3813,<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

381424</a>

3815] In addition, PCV chemotherapy has been shown to be predictive in a retrospective analysis of the phase III trials described earlier. Therefore, assessment of these molecular markers may aid management decisions for anaplastic oligodendrogliomas.</p>

3816</section>

3817</section>

3818<section id="_831">

3819<h3 id="_831_toc">

3820Mixed Gliomas Treatment</h3>

3821<p id="_1299" tabindex="-1">

3822Patients with mixed glial tumors, which include oligoastrocytoma (WHO grade II) and anaplastic oligoastrocytoma (WHO grade III), have highly variable prognoses based upon their status of the <em class="gene-name">

3823IDH1</em>

3824 and <em class="gene-name">

3825IDH2</em>

3826 genes and 1p/19q chromosomes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

382727</a>

3828-<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

382929</a>

3830] Therefore, the optimal treatment for these tumors as a group is uncertain. Often, they are treated similarly to astrocytic tumors because a subset of tumors may have outcomes similar to WHO grade III astrocytic or WHO grade IV glioblastoma tumors. Testing for these known, strong, prognostic molecular markers should be performed, which may help to guide the assessment of risk and subsequent management.</p>

3831<p id="_1300" tabindex="-1">

3832Standard treatment options for mixed gliomas include the following:</p>

3833<div class="pdq-content-list">

3834<ol id="_1301">

3835<li>

3836Surgery plus radiation therapy with or without chemotherapy.</li>

3837</ol>

3838</div>

3839<p id="_1302" tabindex="-1">

3840For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/adult-brain-treatment-pdq#_1067">

3841Astrocytic Tumors Treatment</a>

3842 section.</p>

3843</section>

3844<section id="_835">

3845<h3 id="_835_toc">

3846Ependymal Tumors Treatment</h3>

3847<p id="_1303" tabindex="-1">

3848Ependymal tumors (WHO grade I) and ependymomas (WHO grade II)&#8212;i.e., subependymomas and myxopapillary ependymomas&#8212;are often curable.

3849</p>

3850<p id="_1304" tabindex="-1">

3851Standard treatment options for grades I and II ependymal tumors include the following:</p>

3852<div class="pdq-content-list">

3853<ol id="_1305">

3854<li>

3855Surgery alone if the tumor is totally resectable.</li>

3856<li>

3857Surgery followed by radiation therapy to known or suspected residual

3858tumor.</li>

3859</ol>

3860</div>

3861<p id="_1306" tabindex="-1">

3862Patients with anaplastic ependymomas (WHO grade III) have variable prognoses that depend on the location and

3863extent of disease. Frequently, but not invariably, patients with anaplastic ependymomas have

3864worse prognoses than do those patients with lower-grade ependymal tumors.</p>

3865<p id="_1307" tabindex="-1">

3866Standard treatment options for anaplastic ependymomas include the following:</p>

3867<div class="pdq-content-list">

3868<ol id="_1308">

3869<li>

3870Surgery plus radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

387130</a>

3872]</li>

3873</ol>

3874</div>

3875</section>

3876<section id="_844">

3877<h3 id="_844_toc">

3878Embryonal Cell Tumors (Medulloblastomas) Treatment</h3>

3879<p id="_1309" tabindex="-1">

3880Medulloblastoma occurs primarily in children but may also occur in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

388131</a>

3882] For more information, see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq">

3883Childhood Medulloblastoma and Other Central Nervous System Embryonal Tumors Treatment</a>

3884.</p>

3885<p id="_1310" tabindex="-1">

3886Standard treatment options for medulloblastomas include the following:</p>

3887<div class="pdq-content-list">

3888<ol id="_1311">

3889<li>

3890Surgery plus craniospinal radiation therapy for good-risk patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

389132</a>

3892]</li>

3893</ol>

3894</div>

3895<section id="_1312">

3896<h4 id="_1312_toc">

3897Treatment options under clinical evaluation for medulloblastomas</h4>

3898<p id="_1313" tabindex="-1">

3899Treatment options under clinical evaluation include the following:</p>

3900<div class="pdq-content-list">

3901<ol id="_1314">

3902<li>

3903Surgery plus craniospinal radiation therapy and various chemotherapy regimens are being evaluated for poor-risk patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

390432</a>

3905]</li>

3906</ol>

3907</div>

3908</section>

3909</section>

3910<section id="_851">

3911<h3 id="_851_toc">

3912Pineal Parenchymal Tumors Treatment</h3>

3913<p id="_1315" tabindex="-1">

3914Pineocytomas (WHO grade II), pineoblastomas (WHO grade IV), and pineal parenchymal tumors of intermediate differentiation are diverse tumors that require special consideration. Pineocytomas are slow-growing tumors and prognosis varies. </p>

3915<p id="_1316" tabindex="-1">

3916Pineoblastomas grow more rapidly and patients with these tumors have worse prognoses. Pineal parenchymal tumors of intermediate differentiation have unpredictable growth and clinical behavior.</p>

3917<p id="_1317" tabindex="-1">

3918Standard treatment options for pineal parenchymal tumors include the following:</p>

3919<div class="pdq-content-list">

3920<ol id="_1318">

3921<li>

3922Surgery plus radiation therapy for pineocytoma.</li>

3923<li>

3924Surgery plus radiation therapy and chemotherapy for pineoblastoma.

3925</li>

3926</ol>

3927</div>

3928</section>

3929<section id="_855">

3930<h3 id="_855_toc">

3931Meningeal Tumors Treatment</h3>

3932<p id="_1319" tabindex="-1">

3933WHO grade I meningiomas are usually curable when they are resectable. With the increasing use of sensitive neuroimaging tools, there has been more detection of asymptomatic low-grade meningiomas. Most appear to show minimal growth and can often be safely observed while therapy is deferred until growth or the development of symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

393433</a>

3935,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

393634</a>

3937]</p>

3938<p id="_1320" tabindex="-1">

3939Standard treatment options for meningeal tumors include the following:</p>

3940<div class="pdq-content-list">

3941<ol id="_1321">

3942<li>

3943Active surveillance with deferred treatment, especially for incidentally discovered asymptomatic tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

394433</a>

3945,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

394634</a>

3947].</li>

3948<li>

3949Surgery.</li>

3950<li>

3951Stereotactic radiosurgery for tumors smaller than 3 cm.</li>

3952<li>

3953Surgery plus radiation therapy in selected cases, such as for patients with

3954known or suspected residual disease or with recurrence after previous

3955surgery.</li>

3956<li>

3957Fractionated radiation therapy for patients with unresectable tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

395835</a>

3959]</li>

3960</ol>

3961</div>

3962<p id="_1322" tabindex="-1">

3963The prognoses for patients with WHO grade II meningiomas (atypical, clear cell, and chordoid), WHO grade III meningiomas (anaplastic/malignant, rhabdoid, and papillary), and hemangiopericytomas are worse than the prognoses for patients with low-grade meningiomas because complete resections are less commonly feasible, and

3964the proliferative capacity is greater.</p>

3965<p id="_1323" tabindex="-1">

3966Standard treatment options for grades II and III meningiomas and hemangiopericytomas include the following:</p>

3967<div class="pdq-content-list">

3968<ol id="_1324">

3969<li>

3970Surgery plus radiation therapy.</li>

3971</ol>

3972</div>

3973</section>

3974<section id="_682">

3975<h3 id="_682_toc">

3976Germ Cell Tumors Treatment</h3>

3977<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

3978The prognoses and treatment of patients with germ cell tumors&#8212;which include germinomas, embryonal carcinomas, choriocarcinomas, and teratomas&#8212;depend on tumor histology,

3979tumor location, presence and amount of biological markers, and surgical

3980resectability.</p>

3981</section>

3982<section id="_684">

3983<h3 id="_684_toc">

3984Treatment of Tumors of the Sellar Region</h3>

3985<p id="_1325" tabindex="-1">

3986Craniopharyngiomas (WHO grade I) are often curable. </p>

3987<p id="_1326" tabindex="-1">

3988Standard treatment options for craniopharyngiomas include the following:</p>

3989<div class="pdq-content-list">

3990<ol id="_1327">

3991<li>

3992Surgery alone if the tumor is totally resectable.</li>

3993<li>

3994Debulking surgery plus radiation therapy if the tumor is unresectable.</li>

3995</ol>

3996</div>

3997</section>

3998<section id="_1108">

3999<h3 id="_1108_toc">

4000Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Primary CNS Tumors</h3>

4001<p id="_1109" tabindex="-1">

4002Patients who have CNS tumors that are either infrequently curable or unresectable should consider enrollment in clinical trials. Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

4003NCI website</a>

4004.</p>

4005<p id="_1110" tabindex="-1">

4006Heavy-particle radiation, such as proton-beam therapy, carries the theoretical advantage of delivering high doses of ionizing radiation to the tumor bed while sparing surrounding brain tissue. The data are preliminary for this investigational technique and are not widely available.</p>

4007<p id="_1111" tabindex="-1">

4008Novel biological therapies under clinical evaluation for patients with CNS tumors include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

400936</a>

4010]</p>

4011<div class="pdq-content-list">

4012<ul id="_1112">

4013<li>

4014Dendritic cell vaccination.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

401537</a>

4016]</li>

4017<li>

4018Tyrosine kinase receptor inhibitors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

401938</a>

4020]</li>

4021<li>

4022Farnesyl transferase inhibitors.</li>

4023<li>

4024Viral-based gene therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

402539</a>

4026,<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

402740</a>

4028]</li>

4029<li>

4030Oncolytic viruses.</li>

4031<li>

4032Epidermal growth factor-receptor inhibitors.</li>

4033<li>

4034Vascular endothelial growth factor inhibitors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

403536</a>

4036]</li>

4037<li>

4038Other antiangiogenesis agents.</li>

4039</ul>

4040</div>

4041</section>

4042<section id="_TrialSearch_792_sid_4">

4043<h3 id="_TrialSearch_792_sid_4_toc">

4044Current Clinical Trials</h3>

4045<p id="_TrialSearch_792_22" tabindex="-1">

4046Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4047advanced clinical trial search</a>

4048 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4049General information</a>

4050 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

J Neurosurg 105 (4): 538-43, 2006.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17044555&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17044555&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4185[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4186</li>

J Clin Oncol 19 (15): 3547-53, 2001.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11481362&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11481362&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4189[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4190</li>

4191<li id="section_4.36">

4192Fine HA: Promising new therapies for malignant gliomas. Cancer J 13 (6): 349-54, 2007 Nov-Dec.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=18032970&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=18032970&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4193[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4194</li>

4195<li id="section_4.37">

4209[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4210</li>

4211</ol>

4212</div>

4213

4214 </section>

4215

4216

4217

4218 <section id="_1113">

4219

4220 <h2>

4221Treatment of Primary Tumors of the Spinal Axis</h2>

4222

4223 <div id="_section_1113" class="pdq-sections">

4224<p id="_1114" tabindex="-1">

4225Surgery and radiation therapy are the primary modalities used to treat tumors of the spinal axis; therapeutic options vary according to the histology of the tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

42261</a>

4227] The experience with chemotherapy for primary spinal cord tumors is limited; no reports of controlled clinical trials are available for these types of tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

42281</a>

4229,<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

42302</a>

4231] Chemotherapy is indicated for most patients with leptomeningeal involvement from a primary or metastatic tumor and positive cerebrospinal fluid cytology.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

42321</a>

4233] Most patients require treatment with corticosteroids, particularly if they are receiving radiation therapy.</p>

4234<p id="_1115" tabindex="-1">

4235Patients who have spinal axis tumors that are either infrequently curable or unresectable should consider enrollment in clinical trials. Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

4236NCI website</a>

4237.</p>

4238<h6 do-not-show="toc">

4239References</h6>

4240<ol>

4241<li id="section_5.1">

4242Cloughesy T, Selch MT, Liau L: Brain. In: Haskell CM: Cancer Treatment. 5th ed. WB Saunders Co, 2001, pp 1106-42.</li>

4243<li id="section_5.2">

4244Mehta M, Vogelbaum MA, Chang S, et al.: Neoplasms of the central nervous system. In: DeVita VT Jr, Lawrence TS, Rosenberg SA: Cancer: Principles and Practice of Oncology. 9th ed. Lippincott Williams &amp; Wilkins, 2011, pp 1700-49.</li>

4245</ol>

4246</div>

4248 </section>

4300<section id="_1167">

4301<h3 id="_1167_toc">

4302General Information About Metastatic Brain Tumors</h3>

4303<p id="_1168" tabindex="-1">

4304Brain metastases outnumber primary neoplasms by at least 10 to 1, and they occur in 20% to 40% of cancer patients, with subsequent median survival generally less than 6 months.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

43051</a>

4306] The exact incidence is unknown because no national cancer registry documents brain metastases, but it has been estimated that 98,000 to 170,000 new cases are diagnosed in the United States each year.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

43072</a>

4308,<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

43093</a>

4310] This number may be increasing because of the capacity of magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) to detect small metastases and because of prolonged survival resulting from improved systemic therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

43111</a>

4312,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

43132</a>

4314]</p>

4315<p id="_1169" tabindex="-1">

4316The most common primary tumors with brain metastases and the percentage of patients effected are as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

43171</a>

4318,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

43192</a>

4320]</p>

4321<div class="pdq-content-list">

4322<ul id="_1170">

4323<li>

4324Lung (18%&#8211;64%).</li>

4325<li>

4326Breast (2%&#8211;21%).</li>

4327<li>

4328Cancer of unknown primary (1%&#8211;18%).</li>

4329<li>

4330Melanoma (4%&#8211;16%).</li>

4331<li>

4332Colorectal (2%&#8211;12%).</li>

4333<li>

4334Kidney (1%&#8211;8%).</li>

4335</ul>

4336</div>

4337<p id="_1171" tabindex="-1">

4338Eighty percent of brain metastases occur in the cerebral hemispheres, 15% occur in the cerebellum, and 5% occur in the brain stem.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

43392</a>

4340] Metastases to the brain are multiple in more than 70% of cases, but solitary metastases also occur.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

43411</a>

4342]</p>

4343<p id="_1172" tabindex="-1">

4344Brain involvement can occur with cancers of the nasopharyngeal region by direct extension along the cranial nerves or through the foramina at the base of the skull. Dural metastases may constitute as much as 9% of total brain metastases.</p>

4345</section>

4346<section id="_1173">

4347<h3 id="_1173_toc">

4348Clinical Features</h3>

4349<p id="_1174" tabindex="-1">

4350The diagnosis of brain metastases in cancer patients is based on the following:</p>

4351<div class="pdq-content-list">

4352<ul id="_1175">

4353<li>

4354Patient history.</li>

4355<li>

4356Neurological examination.</li>

4357<li>

4358Diagnostic procedures, including a contrast MRI of the brain.</li>

4359</ul>

4360</div>

4361<p id="_1176" tabindex="-1">

4362Patients may describe any of the following:</p>

4363<div class="pdq-content-list">

4364<ul id="_1177">

4365<li>

4366Headaches.</li>

4367<li>

4368Weakness.</li>

4369<li>

4370Seizures.</li>

4371<li>

4372Sensory defects.</li>

4373<li>

4374Gait problems.</li>

4375</ul>

4376</div>

4377<p id="_1178" tabindex="-1">

4378Often, family members or friends may notice the following:</p>

4379<div class="pdq-content-list">

4380<ul id="_1179">

4381<li>

4382 Lethargy.</li>

4383<li>

4384Emotional lability.</li>

4385<li>

4386Personality change.</li>

4387</ul>

4388</div>

4389</section>

4390<section id="_1180">

4391<h3 id="_1180_toc">

4392Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

4393<p id="_1181" tabindex="-1">

4394A physical examination may show objective neurological findings or only minor cognitive changes. The presence of multiple lesions and a high predilection of primary tumor metastasis may be sufficient to make the diagnosis of brain metastasis. </p>

4395<p id="_1182" tabindex="-1">

4396A lesion in the brain should not be assumed to be a metastasis just because a

4397patient has had a previous cancer; such an assumption could result in

4398overlooking appropriate treatment of a curable tumor. </p>

4399<section id="_1184">

4400<h4 id="_1184_toc">

4401Imaging tests</h4>

4402<p id="_1185" tabindex="-1">

4403Computed tomography scans with contrast or MRIs with gadolinium are quite sensitive in diagnosing the presence of metastases. Positron emission tomography scanning and spectroscopic evaluation are new strategies to diagnose cerebral metastases and to differentiate the metastases from other intracranial lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

44044</a>

4405]</p>

4406</section>

4407<section id="_1186">

4408<h4 id="_1186_toc">

4409Biopsy</h4>

4410<p id="_1187" tabindex="-1">

4411In the case of a solitary lesion or a questionable relationship to the primary tumor, a brain biopsy (via resection or stereotactic biopsy) may be necessary. </p>

4412</section>

4413</section>

4414<section id="_1188">

4415<h3 id="_1188_toc">

4416Treatment of Metastatic Brain Tumors</h3>

4417<p id="_1189" tabindex="-1">

4418The optimal therapy for patients with brain metastases continues to evolve.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

44191</a>

4420,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

44212</a>

4422,<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

44235</a>

4424] The following treatments have been used in the management of metastatic brain tumors: </p>

4425<div class="pdq-content-list">

4426<ul id="_1190">

4427<li>

4428Radiation therapy.</li>

4429<li>

4430Radiosurgery.</li>

4431<li>

4432Surgical resection.</li>

4433<li>

4434Corticosteroids.</li>

4435<li>

4436Anticonvulsants.</li>

4437</ul>

4438</div>

4439<p id="_1191" tabindex="-1">

4440Because most cases of brain metastases involve multiple metastases, a mainstay of therapy has historically been whole-brain radiation therapy (WBRT); however, stereotactic radiosurgery has come into increasingly common use. The role of radiosurgery continues to be defined. Stereotactic radiosurgery in combination with WBRT has been assessed.</p>

4441<p id="_1328" tabindex="-1">

4442Surgery is indicated to obtain tissue from a metastasis with an unknown primary tumor or to decompress a symptomatic dominant lesion that is causing significant mass effect.</p>

4443<p id="_1192" tabindex="-1">

4444Chemotherapy is usually not the primary therapy for most patients; however, it may have a role in the treatment of patients with brain metastases from chemosensitive tumors and can even be curative when combined with radiation for metastatic testicular germ cell tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

44451</a>

4446,<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

44476</a>

4448] Intrathecal chemotherapy is also used for meningeal spread of metastatic tumors.</p>

4449<section id="_1193">

4450<h4 id="_1193_toc">

4451Treatment for patients with one to four metastases</h4>

4452<section id="_1194">

4453<h5 id="_1194_toc">

4454Standard treatment options for patients with one to four metastases</h5>

4455<p id="_1230" tabindex="-1">

4456About 10% to 15% of patients with cancer will have a single brain metastasis. Radiation therapy is the mainstay of palliation for these patients. The extent of extracranial disease can influence treatment of the brain lesions. In the presence of extensive active systemic disease, surgery provides little benefit for overall survival (OS). In patients with stable minimal extracranial disease, combined modality treatment may be considered, using surgical resection followed by radiation therapy. However, the published literature does not provide clear guidance.</p>

4457<p id="_1195" tabindex="-1">

4458Standard treatment options for patients with one to four metastases include the following:</p>

4459<div class="pdq-content-list">

4460<ol id="_1196">

4461<li>

4462WBRT with or without surgical resection.</li>

4463<li>

4464WBRT with or without stereotactic radiosurgery.</li>

4465<li>

4466Focal therapy alone (surgical resection or stereotactic radiosurgery).</li>

4467</ol>

4468</div>

4469<p id="_1202" tabindex="-1">

4470Evidence (treatment for one to four metastases):</p>

4471<div class="pdq-content-list">

4472<ol id="_1200">

4473<li>

4474Three randomized trials examined resection of solitary brain metastases followed by WBRT versus WBRT alone, totaling 195 randomly assigned patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

44757</a>

4476-<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

44779</a>

4478] The process that necessarily goes into selecting appropriate patients for surgical resection may account for the small numbers in each trial. In the first trial,[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

44797</a>

4480][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4481Level of evidence B1</a>

4482] performed at a single center, all patients were selected and operated upon by one surgeon. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4483<ol id="_1329" class="lower-alpha">

4484<li>

4485The first two trials showed an improvement in survival in the surgery group,[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

44867</a>

4487,<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

44888</a>

4489] but the third trial showed a trend in favor of the WBRT&#8211;only group.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

44909</a>

4491]</li>

4492<li>

4493The three trials were combined in a trial-level meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

449410</a>

4495] The combined analysis showed the following: <div class="pdq-content-list">

4496<ul id="_1330">

4497<li>

4498 The combined analysis did not show a statistically significant difference in OS (hazard ratio [HR], 0.72; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.34&#8211;1.53; <em>

4499P</em>

4500 = .4); or in death from neurological causes (relative risk<sub>

4501death</sub>

4502, 0.68; 95% CI, 0.43&#8211;1.09; <em>

4503P</em>

4504 = .11).[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

450510</a>

4506]</li>

4507<li>

4508One of the trials reported that combined therapy increased the duration of functionally independent survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

45097</a>

4510][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4511Level of evidence B1</a>

4512] </li>

4513<li>

4514None of the trials assessed or reported quality of life. </li>

4515</ul>

4516</div>

4517</li>

4518</ol>

4519</div>

4520</li>

4521<li>

4522The need for WBRT after resection of solitary brain metastases has been studied.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

452311</a>

4524] Patients were randomly assigned to either undergo postoperative WBRT or receive no further treatment after resection.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4525<ul id="_1204">

4526<li>

4527Patients in the WBRT group were less likely to have tumor progression in the brain and were significantly less likely to die of neurological causes.</li>

4528<li>

4529OS was the same in each group, and there was no difference in duration of functional independence.</li>

4530</ul>

4531</div>

4532</li>

4533<li>

4534One additional randomized study of observation versus WBRT after either surgery or stereotactic radiosurgery for solitary brain metastases was closed after 19 patients had been entered because of slow accrual; therefore, little can be deduced from the trial.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

453512</a>

4536]</li>

4537<li>

4538A Radiation Therapy Oncology Group (RTOG) study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002708">

4539RTOG-9508</a>

4540) randomly assigned 333 patients with one to three metastases with a maximum diameter of 4 cm to WBRT (37.5 Gy over 3 weeks) with or without a stereotactic boost.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

454113</a>

4542] Patients with active systemic disease requiring therapy were excluded. The primary end point was OS with predefined hypotheses in both the full study population and the 186 patients with a solitary metastasis (and no statistical adjustment of <em>

4543P</em>

4544 values for the two separate hypotheses).[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

454513</a>

4546][Levels of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4547B1</a>

4548 for the full study population and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4549A1</a>

4550 for patients with solitary metastases]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4551<ol id="_1337" class="lower-alpha">

4552<li>

4553 Mean OS in the combined-therapy group was 5.7 months, and mean OS in the WBRT&#8211;alone group was 6.5 months (<em>

4554P</em>

4555 = .14).<div class="pdq-content-list">

4556<ul id="_1338">

4557<li>

4558In the subgroup with solitary metastases, OS was better in the combined-therapy group (6.5 months vs. 4.9 months; <em>

4559P</em>

4560 = .039 in univariate analysis; <em>

4561P</em>

4562 = .053 in a multivariable analysis adjusting for baseline prognostic factors). </li>

4563<li>

4564In patients with multiple metastases, survival was 5.8 months in the combined-therapy group versus 6.7 months in the WBRT&#8211;only group (<em>

4565P</em>

4566 = .98). </li>

4567<li>

4568The combined-treatment group had a survival advantage of 2.5 months in patients with a single metastasis but not in patients with multiple lesions. </li>

4569</ul>

4570</div>

4571</li>

4572<li>

4573Local control was better in the full population with combined therapy.</li>

4574<li>

4575At the 6-month follow-up, Karnofsky Performance status (considered a soft end point because of its imprecision and subjectivity) was better in the combined-therapy group, but there was no difference in mental status between the treatment groups. Acute and late toxicities were similar in both treatment arms. Quality of life was not assessed.</li>

4576</ol>

4577</div>

4578</li>

4579<li>

4580A phase III randomized trial compared adjuvant WBRT with observation after surgery or radiosurgery for a limited number of brain metastases in patients with stable solid tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

458114</a>

4582][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4583Level of evidence A3</a>

4584]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4585<ul id="_1234">

4586<li>

4587Health-related quality of life was improved in the observation-only arm, compared with WBRT. </li>

4588<li>

4589Patients in the observation arm had better mean scores in physical, role, and cognitive functioning at 9 months. </li>

4590<li>

4591In an exploratory analysis, statistically significant worse scores for bladder control, communication deficit, drowsiness, hair loss, motor dysfunction, leg weakness, appetite loss, constipation, nausea/vomiting, pain, and social functioning were observed in patients who underwent WBRT, compared with those who underwent observation only.</li>

4592</ul>

4593</div>

4594</li>

4595<li>

4596A meta-analysis of two trials with a total of 358 participants found no statistically significant difference in OS between the WBRT plus stereotactic radiosurgery group and the WBRT&#8211;alone group (HR, 0.82; 95% CI, 0.65&#8211;1.02).[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

459715</a>

4598][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4599Level of evidence B1</a>

4600]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4601<ul id="_1235">

4602<li>

4603Patients in the WBRT plus stereotactic radiosurgery group had decreased local failure, compared with patients who received WBRT alone (HR, 0.27; 95% CI, 0.14&#8211;0.52).</li>

4604<li>

4605Unchanged or improved Karnofsky Performance status at 6 months was seen in 43% of patients in the combined-therapy group versus only 28% in the WBRT-alone group (<em>

4606P</em>

4607 = .03).</li>

4608</ul>

4609</div>

4610 </li>

4611</ol>

4612</div>

4613<p id="_1212" tabindex="-1">

4614A study that had a primary end point of learning and neurocognition, using a standardized test for total recall, was stopped by the Data and Safety Monitoring Board because of worse outcomes in the WBRT group.[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

461516</a>

4616][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4617Level of evidence B1</a>

4618] </p>

4619<p id="_1331" tabindex="-1">

4620Given this body of information, focal therapy plus WBRT or focal therapy alone, with close follow-up with serial MRIs and initiation of salvage therapy when clinically indicated, appear to be reasonable treatment options. The pros and cons of each approach should be discussed with the patient.</p>

4621<p id="_1213" tabindex="-1">

4622Several randomized trials have been performed that were designed with varying primary end points to address whether WBRT is necessary after focal treatment. The results can be summarized as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

462316</a>

4624-<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

462518</a>

4626]</p>

4627<div class="pdq-content-list">

4628<ol id="_1214">

4629<li>

4630Studies consistently show that the addition of WBRT to focal therapy decreases the risk of progression and new metastases in the brain.</li>

4631<li>

4632The addition of WBRT does not improve OS.</li>

4633<li>

4634The decrease in risk of intracranial disease progression does not translate into improved functional or neurological status, nor does it appear to decrease the risk of death from neurological deterioration.</li>

4635<li>

4636About half or more of the patients who receive focal therapy alone ultimately require salvage therapy, such as WBRT or radiosurgery, compared with about a quarter of the patients who are given up-front WBRT.</li>

4637<li>

4638The impact of better local control associated with WBRT on quality of life has not been reported and remains an open question.</li>

4639</ol>

4640</div>

4641</section>

4642</section>

4643</section>

4644<section id="_1215">

4645<h3 id="_1215_toc">

4646Leptomeningeal Carcinomatosis (LC)</h3>

4647<p id="_1216" tabindex="-1">

4648LC occurs in about 5% of all cancer patients. The most common types of cancer to spread to the leptomeninges are:</p>

4649<div class="pdq-content-list">

4650<ul id="_1217">

4651<li>

4652Breast tumors (35%).</li>

4653<li>

4654Lung tumors (24%).</li>

4655<li>

4656Hematologic malignancies (16%).</li>

4657</ul>

4658</div>

4659<p id="_1218" tabindex="-1">

4660Diagnosis includes a combination of neurospinal axis imaging and cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) cytology. Median OS is in the range of 10 to 12 weeks.</p>

4661<p id="_1219" tabindex="-1">

4662The management of LC includes the following:</p>

4663<div class="pdq-content-list">

4664<ul id="_1220">

4665<li>

4666Intrathecal chemotherapy.</li>

4667<li>

4668Intrathecal chemotherapy and systemic chemotherapy. </li>

4669<li>

4670Intrathecal chemotherapy and radiation therapy. </li>

4671<li>

4672Supportive care.</li>

4673</ul>

4674</div>

4675<p id="_1221" tabindex="-1">

4676In a series of 149 patients with metastatic non-small cell lung carcinoma, cytologically proven LC, poor performance status, high protein level in the CSF, and a high initial CSF white blood cell count were significant poor prognostic factors for survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

467719</a>

4678] Patients received active treatment, including intrathecal chemotherapy, WBRT, or epidermal growth factor receptor-tyrosine kinase inhibitors, or underwent a ventriculoperitoneal shunt procedure. </p>

4679<p id="_1222" tabindex="-1">

4680In a retrospective series of 38 patients with metastatic breast cancer and LC, the proportion of LC cases varied by breast cancer subtype:[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

468120</a>

4682]</p>

4683<div class="pdq-content-list">

4684<ul id="_1332">

4685<li>

4686Luminal A (18.4%).</li>

4687<li>

4688 Luminal B (31.6%). </li>

4689<li>

4690Human epidermal growth factor receptor 2 (HER2)-positive (26.3%). </li>

4691<li>

4692Triple-negative breast cancer subtype (23.7%).</li>

4693</ul>

4694</div>

4695<p id="_1333" tabindex="-1">

4696Patients with triple-negative breast cancer had a shorter interval between metastatic breast cancer diagnosis and the development of LC. Median survival did not differ across breast cancer subtypes. Consideration of intrathecal administration of trastuzumab in patients with HER2-positive LC has also been described in case reports.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

469721</a>

4698]</p>

4699</section>
